id="cite_ref-Russell2012_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2012-8">[7]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Equatorial <a href="/wiki/Standard_asteroid_physical_characteristics#Surface_gravity" title="Standard asteroid physical characteristics">surface&#160;gravity</a></div></th><td>0.25 <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">m/s<sup>2</sup></a><br/>0.025 <i><a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">g</a></i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Equatorial <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a></div></th><td>0.36 &#160;km/s</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Rotation_period" title="Rotation period">Rotation period</a></div></th><td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7004192326400000000&#9824;</span>0.2226&#160;d</span> (5.342 h)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lc_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-lc-12">[10]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Equatorial rotation&#160;velocity</div></th><td>93.1 m/s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-fact2-13">[11]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">North&#160;pole <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">right ascension</a></span></div></th><td>20h 32m</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">North&#160;pole <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a></div></th><td>48&#176;</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Geometric_albedo" title="Geometric albedo">Geometric albedo</a></div></th><td>0.423<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iras_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-iras-14">[12]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">Temperature</a></th><td><i>min:</i> 85 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> (&#8722;188 &#176;C)<br/><i>max:</i> 270 K (&#8722;3 &#176;C)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mueller01_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller01-15">[13]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Asteroid_spectral_types" title="Asteroid spectral types">Spectral type</a></div></th><td><a href="/wiki/V-type_asteroid" title="V-type asteroid">V</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tax_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-tax-16">[14]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">Apparent magnitude</a></div></th><td>5.1<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pasachoff1983_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pasachoff1983-17">[15]</a></sup> to 8.48</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude#Solar_System_bodies_(H)" title="Absolute magnitude">Absolute magnitude&#160;<i>(H)</i></a></div></th><td>3.20<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iras_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-iras-14">[12]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">Angular diameter</a></div></th><td>0.70&#8243; to 0.22&#8243;</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Vesta</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor-planet_designation" title="Minor-planet designation">minor-planet designation</a>: <b>4 Vesta</b>) is one of the largest objects in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, with a mean <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a> of 525 kilometres (326&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2012_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2012-8">[7]</a></sup> It was discovered by the German astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heinrich_Wilhelm_Olbers" title="Heinrich Wilhelm Olbers">Heinrich Wilhelm Olbers</a> on 29 March 1807<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-6">[5]</a></sup> and is named after <a href="/wiki/Vesta_(mythology)" title="Vesta (mythology)">Vesta</a>, the virgin goddess of home and hearth from <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a>.\n</p><p>Vesta is the second-most-massive and second-largest body in the asteroid belt, after the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASADawnMission_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASADawnMission-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambridgeSS_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-CambridgeSS-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2011_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2011-20">[18]</a></sup> and it contributes an estimated 9% of the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitjeva05_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pitjeva05-21">[19]</a></sup>\nIt is slightly larger than <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">Pallas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carry2009_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carry2009-22">[20]</a></sup> though significantly more massive. Vesta is the only known remaining rocky <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanet</a> (with a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiated interior</a>) of the kind that formed the <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubble_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hubble-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAX_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-MAX-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lastofkind_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-lastofkind-25">[23]</a></sup> Numerous fragments of Vesta were ejected by collisions one and two billion years ago that left two enormous craters occupying much of Vesta\'s southern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jutzi2013_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jutzi2013-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook2014a_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook2014a-27">[25]</a></sup> Debris from these events has fallen to Earth as <a href="/wiki/HED_meteorite" title="HED meteorite">howardite&#8211;eucrite&#8211;diogenite (HED) meteorites</a>, which have been a rich source of information about Vesta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McSween2013_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-McSween2013-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelley2003_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelley2003-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa2011-vesta_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa2011-vesta-30">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Vesta is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brightest_asteroid" title="Brightest asteroid">brightest asteroid</a> visible from Earth. Its maximum distance from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> is slightly greater than the minimum distance of Ceres from the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[c]</a></sup> though its orbit lies entirely within that of Ceres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GravitySim_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-GravitySim-32">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)"><i>Dawn</i> spacecraft</a> entered orbit around Vesta on 16 July 2011 for a one-year exploration and left orbit on 5 September 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawnstatus_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawnstatus-33">[30]</a></sup> en route to its final destination, Ceres. Researchers continue to examine data collected by <i>Dawn</i> for additional insights into the formation and history of Vesta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammannito2013_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ammannito2013-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook2014b_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook2014b-35">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872693506">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Observations_from_Earth_orbit"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Observations from Earth orbit</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Observations_from_Dawn"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Observations from <i>Dawn</i></span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a class="toctext">2012&#8211;2013</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#2014"><span class="tocnumber">10.3</span> <span class="toctext">2014</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg/200px-Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg/300px-Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg/400px-Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Size comparison: the <a href="/wiki/List_of_minor_planets:_1%E2%80%931000" title="List of minor planets: 1&#8211;1000">first 10 asteroid-belt objects</a> profiled against the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Vesta is fourth from the left.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_and_Vesta,_Moon_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/200px-Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/300px-Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/400px-Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_and_Vesta,_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vesta, <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> with sizes shown to scale</div></div></div>\n<p>Heinrich Olbers discovered <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">Pallas</a> in 1802, the year after the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>. He proposed that the two objects were the remnants of a <a href="/wiki/Phaeton_(hypothetical_planet)" title="Phaeton (hypothetical planet)">destroyed planet</a>. He sent a letter with his proposal to the English astronomer <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a>, suggesting that a search near the locations where the orbits of Ceres and Pallas intersected might reveal more fragments. These orbital intersections were located in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cetus" title="Cetus">Cetus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virgo_(constellation)" title="Virgo (constellation)">Virgo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Littmann2004_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Littmann2004-36">[33]</a></sup> Olbers commenced his search in 1802, and on 29 March 1807 he discovered Vesta in the constellation Virgo&#8212;a coincidence, because Ceres, Pallas, and Vesta are not fragments of a larger body. Because the asteroid <a href="/wiki/3_Juno" title="3 Juno">Juno</a> had been discovered in 1804, this made Vesta the fourth object to be identified in the region that is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>. The discovery was announced in a letter addressed to German astronomer <a href="/wiki/Johann_Hieronymus_Schr%C3%B6ter" title="Johann Hieronymus Schr&#246;ter">Johann H. Schr&#246;ter</a> dated 31 March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynn1907_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lynn1907-37">[34]</a></sup> Because Olbers already had credit for discovering a planet (Pallas; at the time, the asteroids were considered to be planets), he gave the honor of naming his new discovery to German mathematician <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a>, whose orbital calculations had enabled astronomers to confirm the existence of Ceres, the first asteroid, and who had computed the orbit of the new planet in the remarkably short time of 10 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunnington2004_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunnington2004-38">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rao2003_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rao2003-39">[36]</a></sup> Gauss decided on the <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman</a> virgin goddess of home and hearth, <a href="/wiki/Vesta_(mythology)" title="Vesta (mythology)">Vesta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPDictionary_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPDictionary-40">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Vesta was the fourth asteroid to be discovered, hence the number 4 in its formal designation. The name <i>Vesta</i>, or national variants thereof, is in international use with two exceptions: <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and China. In <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Greek</a>, the name adopted was the Hellenic equivalent of Vesta, <a href="/wiki/Hestia" title="Hestia">Hestia</a> (<span lang="el" title="Greek language text">4 &#917;&#963;&#964;&#943;&#945;</span>); in English, that name is used for <a href="/wiki/46_Hestia" title="46 Hestia">46 Hestia</a> (Greeks use the name "Hestia" for both, with the minor-planet numbers used for disambiguation). In <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Chinese</a>, Vesta is called the \'hearth-god(dess) star\', <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#28790;&#31070;&#26143;</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn" title="Chinese-language transliteration">z&#224;osh&#233;nx&#299;ng</i></i>, naming the asteroid for Vesta\'s role rather than literally transliterating her name into Chinese, as is done with other bodies discovered in modern times, including <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[d]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Upon its discovery, Vesta was, like Ceres, Pallas, and Juno before it, classified as a planet and given a <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbols" title="Astronomical symbols">planetary symbol</a>. The symbol representing the altar of Vesta with its <a href="/wiki/Sacred_fire_of_Vesta" title="Sacred fire of Vesta">sacred fire</a> and was designed by Gauss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcbeh-v15_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcbeh-v15-42">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-effemeridi-1809_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-effemeridi-1809-43">[39]</a></sup> In Gauss\'s conception, this was drawn <span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Simbolo_di_Vesta.svg" title="Gauss\'s version of the astronomical symbol for Vesta"><img alt="Gauss\'s version of the astronomical symbol for Vesta" data-file-height="98" data-file-width="99" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Simbolo_di_Vesta.svg/15px-Simbolo_di_Vesta.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Simbolo_di_Vesta.svg/23px-Simbolo_di_Vesta.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Simbolo_di_Vesta.svg/30px-Simbolo_di_Vesta.svg.png 2x" width="15"/></a>;</span> in its modern form, it is <span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_symbol.svg" title="modern version of the astronomical symbol for Vesta"><img alt="modern version of the astronomical symbol for Vesta" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Vesta_symbol.svg/20px-Vesta_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Vesta_symbol.svg/30px-Vesta_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Vesta_symbol.svg/40px-Vesta_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[e]</a></sup></span>\n</p><p>After the discovery of Vesta, no further objects were discovered for 38 years, and the Solar System was thought to have eleven planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18planets_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-18planets-47">[42]</a></sup> However, in 1845, new asteroids started being discovered at a rapid pace, and by 1851 there were fifteen, each with its own symbol, in addition to the eight major planets (<a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> had been discovered in 1846). It soon became clear that it would be impractical to continue inventing new planetary symbols indefinitely, and some of the existing ones proved difficult to draw quickly. That year, the problem was addressed by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Apthorp_Gould" title="Benjamin Apthorp Gould">Benjamin Apthorp Gould</a>, who suggested numbering asteroids in their order of discovery, and placing this number in a disk (circle) as the generic symbol of an asteroid. Thus, the fourth asteroid, Vesta, acquired the generic symbol &#9315;. This was soon coupled with the name into an official number&#8211;name designation, <i>&#9315; Vesta</i>, as the number of minor planets increased. By 1858, the circle had been simplified to parentheses, <i>(4) Vesta</i>, which were easier to typeset. Other punctuation, such as <i>4) Vesta</i> and <i>4, Vesta</i>, was also used, but had more or less completely died out by 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WhenbecameMPs_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-WhenbecameMPs-48">[43]</a></sup> Today, either <i>Vesta</i>, or, more commonly, <i>4 Vesta</i>, is used.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_measurements">Early measurements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early measurements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_SPHERE_view_of_Vesta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1840" data-file-width="3618" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/New_SPHERE_view_of_Vesta.jpg/220px-New_SPHERE_view_of_Vesta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/New_SPHERE_view_of_Vesta.jpg/330px-New_SPHERE_view_of_Vesta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/New_SPHERE_view_of_Vesta.jpg/440px-New_SPHERE_view_of_Vesta.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_SPHERE_view_of_Vesta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Spectro-Polarimetric_High-Contrast_Exoplanet_Research" title="Spectro-Polarimetric High-Contrast Exoplanet Research">SPHERE</a> image is shown on the left, with a synthetic view derived from space-based data shown on the right for comparison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Photometric observations of Vesta were made at the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College_Observatory" title="Harvard College Observatory">Harvard College Observatory</a> in 1880&#8211;1882 and at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observatoire_de_Toulouse" title="Observatoire de Toulouse">Observatoire de Toulouse</a> in 1909. These and other observations allowed the rotation rate of Vesta to be determined by the 1950s. However, the early estimates of the rotation rate came into question because the <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a> included variations in both shape and <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFadden2008_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-McFadden2008-50">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early estimates of the diameter of Vesta ranged from 383 (in 1825) to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005444000000000000&#9824;</span>444&#160;km</span>. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Charles_Pickering" title="Edward Charles Pickering">E.C. Pickering</a> produced an estimated diameter of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005513000000000000&#9824;</span>513<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>17&#160;km</span> in 1879, which is close to the modern value for the mean diameter, but the subsequent estimates ranged from a low of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005390000000000000&#9824;</span>390&#160;km</span> up to a high of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005602000000000000&#9824;</span>602&#160;km</span> during the next century. The measured estimates were based on <a href="/wiki/Photometry_(astronomy)" title="Photometry (astronomy)">photometry</a>. In 1989, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speckle_interferometry" title="Speckle interferometry">speckle interferometry</a> was used to measure a dimension that varied between 498 and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005548000000000000&#9824;</span>548&#160;km</span> during the rotational period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes1994_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes1994-51">[46]</a></sup> In 1991, an <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a> of the star <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SAO_93228&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SAO 93228 (page does not exist)">SAO 93228</a> by Vesta was observed from multiple locations in the eastern United States and Canada. Based on observations from 14 different sites, the best fit to the data was an elliptical profile with dimensions of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005550000000000000&#9824;</span>550&#160;km</span> &#215; <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005462000000000000&#9824;</span>462&#160;km</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Povenmire2001_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Povenmire2001-52">[47]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> confirmed this measurement.\n</p><p>Vesta became the first asteroid to have its mass determined. Every 18 years, the asteroid <a href="/wiki/197_Arete" title="197 Arete">197 Arete</a> approaches within <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009598391482800000&#9824;</span>0.04&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a></span> of Vesta. In 1966, based upon observations of Vesta\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_perturbation" title="Gravitational perturbation">gravitational perturbations</a> of Arete, Hans G. Hertz estimated the mass of Vesta as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990120000000000000&#9824;</span>(1.20<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.08)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;10</sup></span> <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science160_3825_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-science160_3825-53">[48]</a></sup> More refined estimates followed, and in 2001 the perturbations of <a href="/wiki/17_Thetis" title="17 Thetis">17 Thetis</a> were used to estimate the mass of Vesta as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990131000000000000&#9824;</span>(1.31<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.02)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;10</sup></span> solar masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa430_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa430-54">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit">Orbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Orbit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Vesta orbits the Sun between Mars and Jupiter, within the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, with a period of 3.6 Earth years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-6">[5]</a></sup> specifically in the inner asteroid belt, interior to the <a href="/wiki/Kirkwood_gap" title="Kirkwood gap">Kirkwood gap</a> at 2.50&#160;AU. Its orbit is moderately inclined (<i>i</i> = 7.1&#176;, compared to 7&#176; for <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> and 17&#176; for <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>) and moderately <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> (<i>e</i> = 0.09, about the same as for Mars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-6">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>True <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonances</a> between asteroids are considered unlikely; due to their small masses relative to their large separations, such relationships should be very rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christou_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christou-55">[50]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Vesta is able to capture other asteroids into temporary 1:1 resonant orbital relationships (for periods up to 2 million years or more); about forty such objects have been identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christou2012_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christou2012-56">[51]</a></sup> Decameter-sized objects detected in the vicinity of Vesta by <i>Dawn</i> may be such <a href="/wiki/Quasi-satellite" title="Quasi-satellite">quasi-satellites</a> rather than proper satellites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christou2012_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Christou2012-56">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotation">Rotation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Rotation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:588px;max-width:588px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Olbers_Regio.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="142" data-file-width="134" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/be/Olbers_Regio.gif" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Olbers Regio (dark area) defines the prime meridian in the IAU coordinate system. It is shown here in a <i>Hubble</i> shot of Vesta, because it is not visible in the more detailed <i>Dawn</i> images.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:382px;max-width:382px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Claudia_crater,_Vesta_IOTD-298.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="2350" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Claudia_crater%2C_Vesta_IOTD-298.jpg/380px-Claudia_crater%2C_Vesta_IOTD-298.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Claudia_crater%2C_Vesta_IOTD-298.jpg/570px-Claudia_crater%2C_Vesta_IOTD-298.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Claudia_crater%2C_Vesta_IOTD-298.jpg/760px-Claudia_crater%2C_Vesta_IOTD-298.jpg 2x" width="380"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Claudia_(crater)" title="Claudia (crater)">Claudia crater</a> (indicated by the arrow at the bottom of the closeup image at right) defines the prime meridian in the <i>Dawn</i>/NASA coordinate system.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Vesta\'s rotation is relatively fast for an asteroid (5.342&#160;h) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">prograde</a>, with the north pole pointing in the direction of <a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">right ascension</a> 20&#160;h&#160;32&#160;min, <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a> +48&#176; (in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(constellation)" title="Cygnus (constellation)">Cygnus</a>) with an uncertainty of about 10&#176;. This gives an <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> of 29&#176;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas1997b_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas1997b-57">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordinate_systems">Coordinate systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Coordinate systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There are two longitudinal coordinate systems in use for Vesta, with <a href="/wiki/Prime_meridian" title="Prime meridian">prime meridians</a> separated by 150&#176;. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAU" title="IAU">IAU</a> established a coordinate system in 1997 based on <i>Hubble</i> photos, with the prime meridian running through the center of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Olbers_Regio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Olbers Regio (page does not exist)">Olbers Regio</a>, a dark feature 200&#160;km across. When <i>Dawn</i> arrived at Vesta, mission scientists found that the location of the pole assumed by the IAU was off by 10&#176;, so that the IAU coordinate system drifted across the surface of Vesta at 0.06&#176; per year, and also that Olbers Regio was not discernible from up close, and so was not adequate to define the prime meridian with the precision they needed. They corrected the pole, but also established a new prime meridian 4&#176; from the center of <a href="/wiki/Claudia_(crater)" title="Claudia (crater)">Claudia</a>, a sharply defined crater 700 meters across, which they say results in a more logical set of mapping quadrangles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Claudian_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Claudian-58">[53]</a></sup> All NASA publications, including images and maps of Vesta, use the Claudian meridian, which is unacceptable to the IAU. The IAU Working Group on Cartographic Coordinates and Rotational Elements recommended a coordinate system, correcting the pole but rotating the Claudian longitude by 150&#176; to coincide with Olbers Regio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUcoordinates_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAUcoordinates-59">[54]</a></sup> It was accepted by the IAU, though it disrupts the maps prepared by the <i>Dawn</i> team, which had been positioned so they would not bisect any major surface features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Claudian_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Claudian-58">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawncoordinates_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawncoordinates-60">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_full_mosaic.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="767" data-file-width="894" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Vesta_full_mosaic.jpg/300px-Vesta_full_mosaic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Vesta_full_mosaic.jpg/450px-Vesta_full_mosaic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Vesta_full_mosaic.jpg/600px-Vesta_full_mosaic.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_full_mosaic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Composite greyscale image of Vesta taken by <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)"><i>Dawn</i></a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iau_dozen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="888" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Iau_dozen.jpg/220px-Iau_dozen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Iau_dozen.jpg/330px-Iau_dozen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Iau_dozen.jpg/440px-Iau_dozen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iau_dozen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet#First_draft_proposal" title="IAU definition of planet">IAU 2006 draft proposal</a> on the definition of a planet listed Vesta as a candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-draft2006_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-draft2006-61">[56]</a></sup> Vesta is shown fourth from the left along the bottom row.</div></div></div>\n<p>Vesta is the second-most-massive body in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baer2007_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baer2007-62">[57]</a></sup> though only 28% as massive as Ceres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitjeva05_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pitjeva05-21">[19]</a></sup> Vesta\'s density is lower than those of the four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>, but higher than that of most asteroids and all of the moons in the Solar System except <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>. Vesta\'s surface area is about the same as that of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> (about 800,000 square kilometers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[f]</a></sup> It has a differentiated interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubble_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hubble-23">[21]</a></sup> Vesta is only slightly larger (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005525400000000000&#9824;</span>525.4<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.2&#160;km</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2012_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2012-8">[7]</a></sup>) than <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">2 Pallas</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005512000000000000&#9824;</span>512<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>3&#160;km</span>) in volume,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carry2009_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carry2009-22">[20]</a></sup> but is about 25% more massive.\n</p><p>Vesta\'s shape is close to a gravitationally relaxed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate spheroid</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas1997b_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas1997b-57">[52]</a></sup> but the large concavity and protrusion at the southern pole (see \'<a href="#Surface_features">Surface features</a>\' below) combined with a mass less than <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7020500000000000000&#9824;</span>5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>20</sup>&#160;kg</span> precluded Vesta from automatically being considered a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> under <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet#Draft_proposal" title="IAU definition of planet">International Astronomical Union (IAU) Resolution XXVI 5</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU0601_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU0601-64">[58]</a></sup> A 2012 analysis of Vesta\'s shape<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fu_2013_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fu_2013-65">[59]</a></sup> and gravity field using data gathered by the <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)"><i>Dawn</i> spacecraft</a> has shown that Vesta is currently not in <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asmar_2012_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asmar_2012-66">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russel_2012_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russel_2012-67">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Temperatures on the surface have been estimated to lie between about &#8722;20&#160;&#176;C with the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> overhead, dropping to about &#8722;190&#160;&#176;C at the winter pole. Typical daytime and nighttime temperatures are &#8722;60&#160;&#176;C and &#8722;130&#160;&#176;C respectively. This estimate is for 6 May 1996, very close to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>, although details vary somewhat with the seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mueller01_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller01-15">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="margin:0.5em auto; width:600px;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Geologic map of Vesta<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2014_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2014-68">[62]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="font-size:88%"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg"><img alt="PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg" data-file-height="1545" data-file-width="3075" decoding="async" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg/600px-PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg/900px-PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg/1200px-PIA18788-VestaAsteroid-GeologicMap-DawnMission-20141117.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a>\n<center>The most ancient and heavily cratered regions are brown; areas modified by the <a href="/wiki/Veneneia" title="Veneneia">Veneneia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rheasilvia" title="Rheasilvia">Rheasilvia</a> impacts are purple (the Saturnalia Fossae Formation, in the north)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scully2014_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scully2014-69">[63]</a></sup> and light cyan (the Divalia Fossae Formation, equatorial),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2014_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2014-68">[62]</a></sup> respectively; the Rheasilvia impact basin interior (in the south) is dark blue, and neighboring areas of Rheasilvia ejecta (including an area within Veneneia) are light purple-blue;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch&#228;fer2014_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sch&#228;fer2014-70">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kneissl2014_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kneissl2014-71">[65]</a></sup> areas modified by more recent impacts or mass wasting are yellow/orange or green, respectively.</center>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_features">Surface features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Surface features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Vesta" title="List of geological features on Vesta">List of geological features on Vesta</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_northern_and_southern_hemispheres_pia15677.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1996" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Vesta_northern_and_southern_hemispheres_pia15677.jpg/220px-Vesta_northern_and_southern_hemispheres_pia15677.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Vesta_northern_and_southern_hemispheres_pia15677.jpg/330px-Vesta_northern_and_southern_hemispheres_pia15677.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Vesta_northern_and_southern_hemispheres_pia15677.jpg/440px-Vesta_northern_and_southern_hemispheres_pia15677.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_northern_and_southern_hemispheres_pia15677.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Northern (left) and southern (right) hemispheres. The \'Snowman\' craters are at the top of the left image; Rheasilvia and Veneneia (green and blue) dominate the right. Parallel troughs are seen in both. Colors of the two hemispheres are not to scale,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[g]</a></sup> and the equatorial region is not shown.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viewing_the_South_Pole_of_Vesta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="3840" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Viewing_the_South_Pole_of_Vesta.jpg/220px-Viewing_the_South_Pole_of_Vesta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Viewing_the_South_Pole_of_Vesta.jpg/330px-Viewing_the_South_Pole_of_Vesta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Viewing_the_South_Pole_of_Vesta.jpg/440px-Viewing_the_South_Pole_of_Vesta.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viewing_the_South_Pole_of_Vesta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>South pole of Vesta, showing the extent of Rheasilvia crater.</div></div></div>\n<p>Prior to the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)"><i>Dawn</i> spacecraft</a>, some Vestan surface features had already been resolved using the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> and ground-based telescopes (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/W._M._Keck_Observatory" title="W. M. Keck Observatory">Keck Observatory</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zellner2005_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zellner2005-73">[66]</a></sup> The arrival of <i>Dawn</i> in July 2011 revealed the complex surface of Vesta in detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaumann2012_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jaumann2012-74">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rheasilvia_and_Veneneia_craters">Rheasilvia and Veneneia craters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Rheasilvia and Veneneia craters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rheasilvia" title="Rheasilvia">Rheasilvia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veneneia" title="Veneneia">Veneneia</a></div>\n<p>The most prominent of these surface features are two enormous craters, the 500-kilometre (311&#160;mi)-wide Rheasilvia crater, centered near the south pole, and the 400 kilometres (249&#160;mi) wide Veneneia crater. The Rheasilvia crater is younger and overlies the Veneneia crater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2012_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schenk2012-75">[68]</a></sup> The <i>Dawn</i> science team named the younger, more prominent crater <a href="/wiki/Rheasilvia" title="Rheasilvia">Rheasilvia</a>, after the mother of Romulus and Remus and a mythical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vestal_virgin" title="Vestal virgin">vestal virgin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rheasilvianamed_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rheasilvianamed-76">[69]</a></sup> Its width is 95% of the mean diameter of Vesta. The crater is about 19 kilometres (12&#160;mi) deep. A central peak rises 23&#160;km above the lowest measured part of the crater floor and the highest measured part of the crater rim is 31&#160;km above the crater floor low point. It is estimated that the impact responsible excavated about 1% of the volume of Vesta, and it is likely that the <a href="/wiki/Vesta_family" title="Vesta family">Vesta family</a> and <a href="/wiki/V-type_asteroid" title="V-type asteroid">V-type asteroids</a> are the products of this collision. If this is the case, then the fact that 10-km fragments have survived bombardment until the present indicates that the crater is at most only about 1&#160;billion years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binzel1997_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Binzel1997-77">[70]</a></sup> It would also be the site of origin of the <a href="/wiki/HED_meteorite" title="HED meteorite">HED meteorites</a>. All the known V-type asteroids taken together account for only about 6% of the ejected volume, with the rest presumably either in small fragments, ejected by approaching the 3:1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kirkwood_gap" title="Kirkwood gap">Kirkwood gap</a>, or perturbed away by the <a href="/wiki/Yarkovsky_effect" title="Yarkovsky effect">Yarkovsky effect</a> or <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a>. <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">Spectroscopic</a> analyses of the Hubble images have shown that this crater has penetrated deep through several distinct layers of the crust, and possibly into the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>, as indicated by spectral signatures of <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas1997b_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas1997b-57">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The large peak at the center of Rheasilvia is 20&#160;to 25 kilometres (12&#8211;16&#160;mi) high and 180 kilometres (110&#160;mi) wide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2012_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schenk2012-75">[68]</a></sup> and is possibly a result of a planetary-scale impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karimi&amp;Dombard16_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karimi&amp;Dombard16-78">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_craters">Other craters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other craters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17661-NASA-DawnMission-Asteroid-Vesta-20131216.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="863" data-file-width="1437" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/PIA17661-NASA-DawnMission-Asteroid-Vesta-20131216.jpg/220px-PIA17661-NASA-DawnMission-Asteroid-Vesta-20131216.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/PIA17661-NASA-DawnMission-Asteroid-Vesta-20131216.jpg/330px-PIA17661-NASA-DawnMission-Asteroid-Vesta-20131216.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/PIA17661-NASA-DawnMission-Asteroid-Vesta-20131216.jpg/440px-PIA17661-NASA-DawnMission-Asteroid-Vesta-20131216.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA17661-NASA-DawnMission-Asteroid-Vesta-20131216.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aelia Crater</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_unnamed_equatorial_basin_on_4_Vesta.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="216" data-file-width="263" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/00/Old_unnamed_equatorial_basin_on_4_Vesta.png/220px-Old_unnamed_equatorial_basin_on_4_Vesta.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/00/Old_unnamed_equatorial_basin_on_4_Vesta.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_unnamed_equatorial_basin_on_4_Vesta.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Feralia Planitia, an old, degraded crater near Vesta\'s equator (green and blue). It is 270 km across and predates Rheasilvia (green at bottom).</div></div></div>\n<p>Several old, degraded craters rival Rheasilvia and Veneneia in size, though none are quite so large. They include <a href="/wiki/Feralia_Planitia" title="Feralia Planitia">Feralia Planitia</a>, shown at right, which is 270&#160;km across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meetingorganizer_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-meetingorganizer-79">[72]</a></sup> More-recent, sharper craters range up to 158 kilometres (98&#160;mi) Varronilla and 196 kilometres (122&#160;mi) Postumia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planetary_Names_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planetary_Names-80">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id=".22Snowman_craters.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id=\'"Snowman_craters"\'>"Snowman craters"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title=\'Edit section: "Snowman craters"\'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The "snowman craters" is an informal name given to a group of three adjacent craters in Vesta\'s northern hemisphere. Their official names from largest to smallest (west to east) are Marcia, Calpurnia, and Minucia. Marcia is the youngest and cross-cuts Calpurnia. Minucia is the oldest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2014_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2014-68">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:636px;max-width:636px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:377px;max-width:377px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_Snowman_craters.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="878" data-file-width="1651" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Vesta_Snowman_craters.jpg/375px-Vesta_Snowman_craters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Vesta_Snowman_craters.jpg/563px-Vesta_Snowman_craters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Vesta_Snowman_craters.jpg/750px-Vesta_Snowman_craters.jpg 2x" width="375"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">"Snowman" craters by <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> from 5,200 km (3,200 mi) in 2011</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:255px;max-width:255px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_Snowman_craters_close-up.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="647" data-file-width="822" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Vesta_Snowman_craters_close-up.jpg/253px-Vesta_Snowman_craters_close-up.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Vesta_Snowman_craters_close-up.jpg/380px-Vesta_Snowman_craters_close-up.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Vesta_Snowman_craters_close-up.jpg/506px-Vesta_Snowman_craters_close-up.jpg 2x" width="253"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Detailed image of the "Snowman" craters</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Troughs">Troughs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Troughs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The majority of the equatorial region of Vesta is sculpted by a series of concentric troughs. The largest is named <a href="/wiki/Divalia_Fossa" title="Divalia Fossa">Divalia Fossa</a> (10&#8211;20&#160;km wide, 465&#160;km long). Despite the fact that Vesta is a one-seventh the size of the Moon, Divalia Fossa dwarfs the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canyon" title="Grand Canyon">Grand Canyon</a>. A second series, inclined to the equator, is found further north. The largest of the northern troughs is named <a href="/wiki/Saturnalia_Fossa" title="Saturnalia Fossa">Saturnalia Fossa</a> (&#8776; 40&#160;km wide, &gt; 370&#160;km long). These troughs are thought to be large-scale <a href="/wiki/Graben" title="Graben">graben</a> resulting from the impacts that created Rheasilvia and Veneneia craters, respectively. They are some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_largest_rifts_and_valleys_in_the_Solar_System" title="List of largest rifts and valleys in the Solar System">longest chasms in the Solar System</a>, nearly as long as <a href="/wiki/Ithaca_Chasma" title="Ithaca Chasma">Ithaca Chasma</a> on <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a>. The troughs may be graben that formed after another asteroid collided with Vesta, a process that can happen only in a body that, like Vesta, is differentiated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buczkowski2012_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buczkowski2012-81">[74]</a></sup> Vesta\'s differentiation is one of the reasons why scientists consider it a protoplanet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stunted_Planet_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stunted_Planet-82">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:783px;max-width:783px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:377px;max-width:377px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Divalia_Fossa_IOTD-260.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Divalia_Fossa_IOTD-260.jpg/375px-Divalia_Fossa_IOTD-260.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Divalia_Fossa_IOTD-260.jpg/563px-Divalia_Fossa_IOTD-260.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Divalia_Fossa_IOTD-260.jpg/750px-Divalia_Fossa_IOTD-260.jpg 2x" width="375"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A section of Divalia Fossa, with parallel troughs to the north and south</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Divalia_Fossa_PIA15673.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="713" data-file-width="1268" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Divalia_Fossa_PIA15673.jpg/400px-Divalia_Fossa_PIA15673.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Divalia_Fossa_PIA15673.jpg/600px-Divalia_Fossa_PIA15673.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Divalia_Fossa_PIA15673.jpg/800px-Divalia_Fossa_PIA15673.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A computer-generated view of a portion of Divalia Fossa</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_composition">Surface composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Surface composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Compositional information from the visible and infrared spectrometer (VIR), gamma-ray and neutron detector (GRaND), and framing camera (FC), all indicate that the majority of the surface composition of Vesta is consistent with the composition of the howardite, eucrite, and diogenite meteorites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeSanctis2012a_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeSanctis2012a-83">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prettyman2012_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prettyman2012-84">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reddy2012_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reddy2012-85">[78]</a></sup> The Rheasilvia region is richest in diogenite, consistent with the Rheasilvia-forming impact excavating material from deeper within Vesta. The presence of olivine within the Rheasilvia region would also be consistent with excavation of mantle material. However, olivine has only been detected in localized regions of the northern hemisphere, not within Rheasilvia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammannito2013_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ammannito2013-34">[31]</a></sup> The origin of this olivine is currently uncertain.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Features_associated_with_volatiles">Features associated with volatiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Features associated with volatiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Pitted terrain has been observed in four craters on Vesta: Marcia, Cornelia, Numisia and Licinia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denevi2012_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denevi2012-86">[79]</a></sup> The formation of the pitted terrain is proposed to be degassing of impact-heated volatile-bearing material. Along with the pitted terrain, curvilinear gullies are found in Marcia and Cornelia craters. The curvilinear gullies end in lobate deposits, which are sometimes covered by pitted terrain, and are proposed to form by the transient flow of liquid water after buried deposits of ice were melted by the heat of the impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scully2014_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scully2014-69">[63]</a></sup> Hydrated materials have also been detected, many of which are associated with areas of dark material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeSanctis2012b_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeSanctis2012b-87">[80]</a></sup> Consequently, dark material is thought to be largely composed of carbonaceous chondrite, which was deposited on the surface by impacts. Carbonaceous chondrites are comparatively rich in mineralogically bound OH.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reddy2012_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reddy2012-85">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vestan_interior_PIA15510.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Vestan_interior_PIA15510.jpg/220px-Vestan_interior_PIA15510.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Vestan_interior_PIA15510.jpg/330px-Vestan_interior_PIA15510.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Vestan_interior_PIA15510.jpg/440px-Vestan_interior_PIA15510.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vestan_interior_PIA15510.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cut-away schematic of Vestan core, mantle, and crust</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MillbillillieMeteorite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/MillbillillieMeteorite.jpg/220px-MillbillillieMeteorite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/MillbillillieMeteorite.jpg/330px-MillbillillieMeteorite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/MillbillillieMeteorite.jpg/440px-MillbillillieMeteorite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MillbillillieMeteorite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eucrite meteorite</div></div></div>\n<p>There is a large collection of potential samples from Vesta accessible to scientists, in the form of over 1200&#160;<a href="/wiki/HED_meteorite" title="HED meteorite">HED meteorites</a> (Vestan <a href="/wiki/Achondrite" title="Achondrite">achondrites</a>), giving insight into Vesta\'s geologic history and structure. <a href="/wiki/NASA_Infrared_Telescope_Facility" title="NASA Infrared Telescope Facility">NASA Infrared Telescope Facility</a> (NASA IRTF) studies of asteroid <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(237442)_1999_TA10" title="(237442) 1999 TA10">(237442) 1999 TA<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">10</span></span></a></span> suggest that it originated from deeper within Vesta than the HED meteorites<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vestainterior_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vestainterior-88">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Vesta is thought to consist of a metallic iron&#8211;nickel <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a> 214&#8211;226&#160;km in diameter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2012_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2012-8">[7]</a></sup> an overlying rocky <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>, with a surface <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a>. From the first appearance of <a href="/wiki/Calcium%E2%80%93aluminium-rich_inclusion" title="Calcium&#8211;aluminium-rich inclusion">calcium&#8211;aluminium-rich inclusions</a> (the first solid matter in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, forming about 4.567&#160;billion years ago), a likely time line is as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghosh1998_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ghosh1998-89">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Righter1997_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Righter1997-90">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drake2001_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drake2001-91">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahijpal2007_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sahijpal2007-92">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta2010_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta2010-93">[86]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Timeline of the evolution of Vesta\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>2&#8211;3&#160;million years\n</th>\n<td>Accretion completed\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>4&#8211;5&#160;million years\n</th>\n<td>Complete or almost complete melting due to <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium-26" title="Aluminium-26"><sup>26</sup>Al</a>, leading to separation of the metal core\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>6&#8211;7&#160;million years\n</th>\n<td>Progressive crystallization of a <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convecting</a> molten <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>. Convection stopped when about 80% of the material had crystallized\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrusive_(geology)" title="Extrusive (geology)">Extrusion</a> of the remaining molten material to form the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a>, either as <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basaltic</a> <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lavas</a> in progressive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eruption" title="Eruption">eruptions</a>, or possibly forming a short-lived <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> ocean.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2">The deeper layers of the crust <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallize</a> to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonic" title="Plutonic">plutonic</a> rocks, whereas older <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basalts" title="Basalts">basalts</a> are <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphosed</a> due to the pressure of newer surface layers.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2">Slow cooling of the interior\n</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>\n<p>Vesta is the only known intact asteroid that has been resurfaced in this manner. Because of this, some scientists refer to Vesta as a protoplanet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-dawn20110329_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-dawn20110329-94">[87]</a></sup> However, the presence of <a href="/wiki/Iron_meteorite" title="Iron meteorite">iron meteorites</a> and achondritic meteorite classes without identified parent bodies indicates that there once were other differentiated <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igneous" title="Igneous">igneous</a> histories, which have since been shattered by impacts.\n</p>\n<dl><dd><table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Composition of the Vestan crust (by depth)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takeda1997_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Takeda1997-95">[88]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td>A <a href="/wiki/Lithification" title="Lithification">lithified</a> <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a>, the source of <a href="/wiki/Howardite" title="Howardite">howardites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breccia" title="Breccia">brecciated</a> <a href="/wiki/Eucrite" title="Eucrite">eucrites</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">Basaltic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lava_flow" title="Lava flow">lava flows</a>, a source of non-cumulate <a href="/wiki/Eucrite" title="Eucrite">eucrites</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Plutonic rocks consisting of <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pigeonite" title="Pigeonite">pigeonite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plagioclase" title="Plagioclase">plagioclase</a>, the source of cumulate <a href="/wiki/Eucrite" title="Eucrite">eucrites</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Plutonic rocks rich in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthopyroxene" title="Orthopyroxene">orthopyroxene</a> with large grain sizes, the source of <a href="/wiki/Diogenite" title="Diogenite">diogenites</a>.\n</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>\n<p>On the basis of the sizes of <a href="/wiki/V-type_asteroid" title="V-type asteroid">V-type asteroids</a> (thought to be pieces of Vesta\'s crust ejected during large impacts), and the depth of Rheasilvia crater (see below), the crust is thought to be roughly 10 kilometres (6&#160;mi) thick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamaguchi1995_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yamaguchi1995-96">[89]</a></sup>\nFindings from the <i>Dawn</i> spacecraft have found evidence that the troughs that wrap around Vesta could be graben formed by impact-induced faulting (see Troughs section above), meaning that Vesta has more complex geology than other asteroids. Vesta could have been classified as a dwarf planet if it had retained a spherical shape, and it has other qualities that lead to the thought it could be a protoplanet. The only thing that knocked it out of the category of a dwarf planet was the formation of two large impact basins at its southern pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2012_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schenk2012-75">[68]</a></sup> At the time of these impacts Vesta was not warm and plastic enough to return to a shape in hydrostatic equilibrium.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regolith">Regolith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Regolith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Vesta\'s surface is covered by <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a> distinct from that found on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> or asteroids such as <a href="/wiki/25143_Itokawa" title="25143 Itokawa">Itokawa</a>. This is because <a href="/wiki/Space_weathering" title="Space weathering">space weathering</a> acts differently. Vesta\'s surface shows no significant trace of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nanophase_iron&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nanophase iron (page does not exist)">nanophase iron</a> because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_impact" title="Asteroid impact">impact</a> speeds on Vesta are too low to make rock melting and vaporization an appreciable process. Instead, regolith evolution is dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brecciation" title="Brecciation">brecciation</a> and subsequent mixing of bright and dark components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pieters2012_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pieters2012-97">[90]</a></sup> The dark component is probably due to the infall of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbonaceous</a> material, whereas the bright component is the original Vesta basaltic soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2012_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2012-98">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fragments">Fragments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fragments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a> are suspected to be fragments of Vesta caused by impacts. The <a href="/wiki/Vesta_family" title="Vesta family">Vestian</a> asteroids and <a href="/wiki/HED_meteorite" title="HED meteorite">HED meteorites</a> are examples. The <a href="/wiki/V-type_asteroid" title="V-type asteroid">V-type asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/1929_Kollaa" title="1929 Kollaa">1929 Kollaa</a> has been determined to have a composition akin to cumulate <a href="/wiki/Eucrite" title="Eucrite">eucrite</a> meteorites, indicating its origin deep within Vesta\'s crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelley2003_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelley2003-29">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Vesta is currently one of only six identified <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> bodies of which we have physical samples, coming from a number of meteorites suspected to be Vestan fragments. It is estimated that 1 out of 16 meteorites originated from Vesta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siliconvalleyastrolecture_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-siliconvalleyastrolecture-99">[92]</a></sup> The other identified Solar System samples are from Earth itself, <a href="/wiki/Martian_meteorite" title="Martian meteorite">meteorites from Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lunar_meteorite" title="Lunar meteorite">meteorites from the Moon</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_return_mission" title="Sample return mission">samples returned</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, the comet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wild_2" title="Wild 2">Wild 2</a>, and the asteroid <a href="/wiki/25143_Itokawa" title="25143 Itokawa">25143 Itokawa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa2011-vesta_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa2011-vesta-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[h]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1147" data-file-width="1500" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg/300px-Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg/400px-Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s conception of <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> orbiting Vesta</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="165"
