srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif/330px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif/440px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i> Dawn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory from 27 September 2007 to 5 October 2018<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Gold; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Cyan; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;4 Vesta </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">1 Ceres</a></span></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif/220px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif/330px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif/440px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory_around_4_Vesta.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory around 4 Vesta from 15 July 2011 to 10 September 2012<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color: Lime ; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;4 Vesta</span></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1981, a proposal for an asteroid mission was submitted to the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA). Named the <b>Asteroidal Gravity Optical and Radar Analysis</b> (<b>AGORA</b>), this <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> was to launch some time in 1990&#8211;1994 and perform two flybys of large asteroids. The preferred target for this mission was Vesta. AGORA would reach the asteroid belt either by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_slingshot" title="Gravitational slingshot">gravitational slingshot</a> trajectory past Mars or by means of a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engine</a>. However, the proposal was refused by the ESA. A joint <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>&#8211;ESA asteroid mission was then drawn up for a <b>Multiple Asteroid Orbiter with Solar Electric Propulsion</b> (<b>MAOSEP</b>), with one of the mission profiles including an orbit of Vesta. NASA indicated they were not interested in an asteroid mission. Instead, the ESA set up a technological study of a spacecraft with an ion drive. Other missions to the asteroid belt were proposed in the 1980s by France, Germany, Italy and the United States, but none were approved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ulivi_harland08_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-ulivi_harland08-101">[93]</a></sup> Exploration of Vesta by fly-by and <a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)" title="Deep Impact (spacecraft)">impacting penetrator</a> was the second main target of the first plan of the multi-aimed Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesta_mission" title="Vesta mission">Vesta mission</a>, developed in cooperation with European countries for realisation in 1991&#8211;1994 but canceled due to the Soviet Union disbanding.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg/200px-PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg/300px-PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg/400px-PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First image of <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> and Vesta) taken from <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>. The image was made by the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a> on 20 April 2014.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the early 1990s, NASA initiated the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Program" title="Discovery Program">Discovery Program</a>, which was intended to be a series of low-cost scientific missions. In 1996, the program\'s study team recommended a mission to explore the asteroid belt using a spacecraft with an ion engine as a high priority. Funding for this program remained problematic for several years, but by 2004 the <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> vehicle had passed its critical design review<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2007_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2007-102">[94]</a></sup> and construction proceeded.\n</p><p>It launched on 27 September 2007 as the first space mission to Vesta. On 3 May 2011, <i>Dawn</i> acquired its first targeting image 1.2 million kilometers from Vesta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pr2011-138_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-pr2011-138-103">[95]</a></sup> On 16 July 2011, NASA confirmed that it received telemetry from <i>Dawn</i> indicating that the spacecraft successfully entered Vesta\'s orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vega2011_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vega2011-104">[96]</a></sup> It was scheduled to orbit Vesta for one year, until July 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawntimeline_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawntimeline-105">[97]</a></sup> <i>Dawn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> arrival coincided with late summer in the southern hemisphere of Vesta, with the large crater at Vesta\'s south pole (<a href="/wiki/Rheasilvia" title="Rheasilvia">Rheasilvia</a>) in sunlight. Because a season on Vesta lasts eleven months, the northern hemisphere, including anticipated compression fractures opposite the crater, would become visible to <i>Dawn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> cameras before it left orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DawnMission_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-DawnMission-106">[98]</a></sup> <i>Dawn</i> left orbit around Vesta on 4 September 2012 <span class="nowrap">11:26 p.m. PDT</span> to travel to <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawndeparture_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawndeparture-107">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA/DLR released imagery and summary information from a survey orbit, two high-altitude orbits (60&#8211;70 m/pixel) and a low-altitude mapping orbit (20 m/pixel), including digital terrain models, videos and atlases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2013_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2013-108">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roatsch2012_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roatsch2012-109">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roatsch2013_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roatsch2013-110">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journeyaboveVesta_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-journeyaboveVesta-111">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dawn_gis_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-dawn_gis-112">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASADawn_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASADawn-113">[105]</a></sup> Scientists also used <i>Dawn</i> to calculate Vesta\'s precise mass and gravity field. The subsequent determination of the J<sub>2</sub> component yielded a core diameter estimate of about 220&#160;km assuming a crustal density similar to that of the HED.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2013_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2013-108">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Dawn</i> data can be accessed by the public at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UCLA" title="UCLA">UCLA</a> website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawnpublic_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawnpublic-114">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observations_from_Earth_orbit">Observations from Earth orbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Observations from Earth orbit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:30px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_spectral_map_HST1994.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Vesta_spectral_map_HST1994.jpg/120px-Vesta_spectral_map_HST1994.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Vesta_spectral_map_HST1994.jpg/180px-Vesta_spectral_map_HST1994.jpg 1.5x, images retrieved during the high-altitude (60&#8211;70 m/pixel) and low-altitude (~20 m/pixel) mapping orbits are available on the <a class="external text" href="http://dawn.jpl.nasa.gov/multimedia/vesta_dawn_gallery_2.asp" rel="nofollow">Dawn Mission website of JPL/NASA</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Visibility">Visibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Visibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asteroid_Vesta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Asteroid_Vesta.jpg/400px-Asteroid_Vesta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Asteroid_Vesta.jpg/600px-Asteroid_Vesta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Asteroid_Vesta.jpg/800px-Asteroid_Vesta.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asteroid_Vesta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Annotated image from Earth\'s surface in June 2007 with (4) Vesta</div></div></div>\n<p>Its size and unusually bright surface make Vesta the brightest asteroid, and it is occasionally visible to the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a> from dark skies (without <a href="/wiki/Light_pollution" title="Light pollution">light pollution</a>). In May and June 2007, Vesta reached a peak <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">magnitude</a> of +5.4, the brightest since 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SkyTelescope2007a_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-SkyTelescope2007a-115">[107]</a></sup> At that time, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_(astronomy)" title="Opposition (astronomy)">opposition</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> were only a few weeks apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SkyTelescope2007b_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-SkyTelescope2007b-116">[108]</a></sup> It was brighter still at its 22 June 2018 opposition, reaching a magnitude of +5.3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup>\nLess favorable oppositions during late autumn 2008 in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> still had Vesta at a magnitude of from +6.5 to +7.3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James2008_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-James2008-118">[110]</a></sup> Even when in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_conjunction" title="Astronomical conjunction">conjunction</a> with the Sun, Vesta will have a magnitude around +8.5; thus from a pollution-free sky it can be observed with <a href="/wiki/Binoculars" title="Binoculars">binoculars</a> even at <a href="/wiki/Elongation_(astronomy)" title="Elongation (astronomy)">elongations</a> much smaller than near opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James2008_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-James2008-118">[110]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="2010.E2.80.932011"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2010&#8211;2011">2010&#8211;2011</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 2010&#8211;2011">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2010, Vesta reached opposition in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Leo_(constellation)" title="Leo (constellation)">Leo</a> on the night of 17&#8211;18 February, at about magnitude 6.1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horizons_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horizons-119">[111]</a></sup> a brightness that makes it visible in binocular range but generally not for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naked-eye_stars" title="Naked-eye stars">naked eye</a>. Under perfect dark sky conditions where all light pollution is absent it might be visible to an experienced observer without the use of a telescope or binoculars. Vesta came to opposition again on 5 August 2011, in the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Capricornus" title="Capricornus">Capricornus</a> at about magnitude 5.6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horizons_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Horizons-119">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPC-eph_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPC-eph-120">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="2012.E2.80.932013"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2012&#8211;2013">2012&#8211;2013</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: 2012&#8211;2013">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Vesta was at opposition again on 9 December 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NightSkyOnline_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-NightSkyOnline-121">[113]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sky_and_Telescope" title="Sky and Telescope">Sky and Telescope</a> magazine, this year Vesta came within about 6 degrees of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_Ceres" title="1 Ceres">1 Ceres</a> during the winter of 2012 and spring 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flanders_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-flanders-122">[114]</a></sup> Vesta orbits the Sun in 3.63 years and Ceres in 4.6 years, so every 17 years Vesta overtakes Ceres (the last overtaking was in 1996).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flanders_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-flanders-122">[114]</a></sup> On December 1, 2012, Vesta had a magnitude of 6.6, but decreasing to 8.4 by May 1, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flanders_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-flanders-122">[114]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2014">2014</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 2014">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Vesta_July_5_2014.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1077" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Ceres_Vesta_July_5_2014.png/220px-Ceres_Vesta_July_5_2014.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Ceres_Vesta_July_5_2014.png/330px-Ceres_Vesta_July_5_2014.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Ceres_Vesta_July_5_2014.png/440px-Ceres_Vesta_July_5_2014.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Vesta_July_5_2014.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Conjunction of Ceres and Vesta on July 5, 2014 in <a href="/wiki/Virgo_(constellation)" title="Virgo (constellation)">Virgo</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Ceres and Vesta came within one degree of each other in the night sky in July 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flanders_122-3"><a href="#cite_note-flanders-122">[114]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul title="List of tallest mountains in the Solar System">List of tallest mountains in the Solar System</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Marc Rayman of the JPL <i>Dawn</i> team used "Vestian" a few times in 2010 and early 2011 in his <i>Dawn Journal</i>. However, since that time, and outside the journal, the form "Vestan" has been used by JPL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DawnMissionSearch_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-DawnMissionSearch-1">[1]</a></sup> Most modern print sources also use "Vestan",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPS_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPS-2">[2]</a></sup> though the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_Society" title="Planetary Society">Planetary Society</a></i> continues to use "Vestian".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlanetarySociety_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlanetarySociety-3">[3]</a></sup> The form "Vestalian" correctly refers to people or things associated with Vesta, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vestal_virgin" title="Vestal virgin">vestal virgins</a>, not to Vesta herself.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-surface-vol-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-surface-vol_9-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-surface-vol_9-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Calculated using the known dimensions assuming an <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoid</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On 10 February 2009, during Ceres <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion</a>, Ceres was closer to the Sun than Vesta, because Vesta has an <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">aphelion</a> distance greater than Ceres\'s perihelion distance. (2009-02-10: Vesta 2.56AU; Ceres 2.54AU)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">&#32173;&#26031;&#22612; <i>w&#233;is&#299;t&#462;</i>, with an obscure <i>&#299;</i>, is the closest Chinese approximation of the Latin pronunciation <i>westa</i>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other sources contemporaneous to Gauss used a more elaborate form of the symbol, <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:4_Vesta_(2).svg" title="Elaborate form of the astronomical symbol for Vesta"><img alt="Elaborate form of the astronomical symbol for Vesta" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="36" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/4_Vesta_%282%29.svg/14px-4_Vesta_%282%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/4_Vesta_%282%29.svg/21px-4_Vesta_%282%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/4_Vesta_%282%29.svg/29px-4_Vesta_%282%29.svg.png 2x" width="14"/></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annuaire-1808_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-annuaire-1808-44">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canovai1810_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canovai1810-45">[41]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Or Venezuela or Tanzania; somewhat larger than Texas and ten times that of the UK.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">that is, blue in the north does not mean the same thing as blue in the south</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note that there is very strong evidence that <a href="/wiki/6_Hebe" title="6 Hebe">6 Hebe</a> is the parent body for H-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chondrites" title="Chondrites">chondrites</a>, one of the most common meteorite types.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=4_Vesta&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em;
